<td>Death</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Death from ethanol consumption is possible when blood alcohol levels reach 0.4%. A blood level of 0.5% or more is commonly fatal. Levels of even less than 0.1% can cause <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_intoxication" title="Alcohol intoxication">intoxication</a>, with unconsciousness often occurring at 0.3–0.4%.<sup id="cite_ref-yost_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-yost-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>Prolonged heavy consumption of alcohol can cause significant permanent damage to the brain and other organs.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Interactions">Interactions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Interactions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Ethanol can intensify the sedation caused by other <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">central nervous system</a> <a href="/wiki/Depressant" title="Depressant">depressant</a> drugs such as <a href="/wiki/Barbiturate" title="Barbiturate">barbiturates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Benzodiazepine" title="Benzodiazepine">benzodiazepines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Opioid" title="Opioid">opioids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Non-benzodiazepine" class="mw-redirect" title="Non-benzodiazepine">non-benzodiazepines</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Zolpidem" title="Zolpidem">Zolpidem</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zopiclone" title="Zopiclone">Zopiclone</a>), <a href="/wiki/Antipsychotics" class="mw-redirect" title="Antipsychotics">antipsychotics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Histamine_antagonist" class="mw-redirect" title="Histamine antagonist">sedative antihistamines</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Antidepressants" class="mw-redirect" title="Antidepressants">antidepressants</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-yost_69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-yost-69">[69]</a></sup> It interacts with <a href="/wiki/Cocaine" title="Cocaine">cocaine</a> in vivo to produce <a href="/wiki/Cocaethylene" title="Cocaethylene">cocaethylene</a>, another psychoactive substance.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> Ethanol enhances the <a href="/wiki/Bioavailability" title="Bioavailability">bioavailability</a> of <a href="/wiki/Methylphenidate" title="Methylphenidate">methylphenidate</a> (elevated plasma d-MPH).<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> In combination with cannabis, ethanol increases plasma THC levels, which suggests that ethanol may increase the absorption of THC.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alcohol_and_metronidazole">Alcohol and metronidazole</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Alcohol and metronidazole">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>One of the most important drug/food interactions that should be noted is between alcohol and <a href="/wiki/Metronidazole" title="Metronidazole">metronidazole</a>.</p>
<p>Metronidazole is an antibacterial agent that kills bacteria by damaging cellular DNA and hence cellular function.<sup id="cite_ref-cps_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cps-73">[73]</a></sup> Metronidazole is usually given to people who have diarrhea caused by <i><a href="/wiki/Clostridium_difficile_(bacteria)" title="Clostridium difficile (bacteria)">Clostridium difficile</a></i> bacteria. <i>C. difficile</i> is one of the most common microorganisms that cause diarrhea and can lead to complications such as colon inflammation and even more severely, death.</p>
<p>Patients who are taking metronidazole are strongly advised to avoid alcohol, even after 1&#160;hour after the last dose. The reason is that alcohol and metronidazole can lead to side effects such as flushing, headache, nausea, vomiting, abdominal cramps, and sweating.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-pharmaceuticals1997_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pharmaceuticals1997-75">[75]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-pharmaceuticals1997_75-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pharmaceuticals1997-75">[75]</a></sup> These symptoms are often called the <a href="/wiki/Disulfiram" title="Disulfiram">disulfiram</a>-like reaction. The proposed mechanism of action for this interaction is that metronidazole can bind to an enzyme that normally metabolizes alcohol. Binding to this enzyme may impair the liver's ability to process alcohol for proper excretion.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pharmacology">Pharmacology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Pharmacology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pharmacodynamics">Pharmacodynamics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Pharmacodynamics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_intoxication#GABAA_receptors" title="Alcohol intoxication">Alcohol intoxication §&#160;GABAA receptors</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Calcium_channel_blocker#Ethanol" title="Calcium channel blocker">Calcium channel blocker §&#160;Ethanol</a></div>
<p>Ethanol acts in the central nervous system primarily by binding to the GABA<sub>A</sub> receptor, increasing the effects of the inhibitory <a href="/wiki/Neurotransmitter" title="Neurotransmitter">neurotransmitter</a> <a href="/wiki/GABA" class="mw-redirect" title="GABA">GABA</a> (i.e., it is a <a href="/wiki/Positive_allosteric_modulator" class="mw-redirect" title="Positive allosteric modulator">positive allosteric modulator</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-pmid17591544_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17591544-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>Ethanol is known to possess the following direct <a href="/wiki/Pharmacodynamic" class="mw-redirect" title="Pharmacodynamic">pharmacodynamic</a> actions (most important actions are bolded):<sup id="cite_ref-pmid19342616_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid19342616-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/GABAA_receptor" title="GABAA receptor">GABA<sub>A</sub> receptor</a> <a href="/wiki/Positive_allosteric_modulator" class="mw-redirect" title="Positive allosteric modulator">positive allosteric modulator</a></b><sup id="cite_ref-NHM-Ethanol_64-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NHM-Ethanol-64">[64]</a></sup> (primarily of <a href="/wiki/GABRD" title="GABRD">δ subunit</a>-containing receptors)</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/NMDA_receptor" title="NMDA receptor">NMDA receptor</a> <a href="/wiki/Negative_allosteric_modulator" class="mw-redirect" title="Negative allosteric modulator">negative allosteric modulator</a></b><sup id="cite_ref-NHM-Ethanol_64-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NHM-Ethanol-64">[64]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-pmid22429661_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid22429661-79">[79]</a></sup>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mesolimbic_pathway" title="Mesolimbic pathway">Mesolimbic</a> <a href="/wiki/Dopamine" title="Dopamine">dopamine</a> release, secondary to GABAA/NMDA receptor modulation<sup id="cite_ref-NHM-Ethanol_64-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NHM-Ethanol-64">[64]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mesolimbic_dopamine_release_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mesolimbic_dopamine_release-61">[61]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Endogenous_opioid" class="mw-redirect" title="Endogenous opioid">Endogenous opioid</a> release in the mesolimbic dopamine pathway, secondary to an unidentified mechanism in the <a href="/wiki/Nucleus_accumbens" title="Nucleus accumbens">NAcc</a> or <a href="/wiki/Ventral_tegmental_area" title="Ventral tegmental area">VTA</a><sup id="cite_ref-NHM-Ethanol_64-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NHM-Ethanol-64">[64]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/AMPA_receptor" title="AMPA receptor">AMPA receptor</a> negative allosteric modulator<sup id="cite_ref-pmid22429661_79-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid22429661-79">[79]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kainate_receptor" title="Kainate receptor">Kainate receptor</a> negative allosteric modulator<sup id="cite_ref-pmid22429661_79-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid22429661-79">[79]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nicotinic_acetylcholine_receptor" title="Nicotinic acetylcholine receptor">nACh receptor</a> agonist</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/5-HT3_receptor" title="5-HT3 receptor">5-HT<sub>3</sub> receptor</a> agonist</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Glycine_reuptake_inhibitor" title="Glycine reuptake inhibitor">Glycine reuptake inhibitor</a><sup id="cite_ref-SitteFreissmuth2006_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SitteFreissmuth2006-80">[80]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Adenosine_reuptake_inhibitor" title="Adenosine reuptake inhibitor">Adenosine reuptake inhibitor</a><sup id="cite_ref-pmid19298329_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid19298329-81">[81]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/L-type_calcium_channel" title="L-type calcium channel">L-type calcium channel</a> <a href="/wiki/Channel_blocker" title="Channel blocker">blocker</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/G_protein-coupled_inwardly-rectifying_potassium_channel" title="G protein-coupled inwardly-rectifying potassium channel">GIRK channel</a> <a href="/wiki/Channel_opener" title="Channel opener">opener</a></li>
</ul>
<p>Some of its actions on <a href="/wiki/Ligand-gated_ion_channel" title="Ligand-gated ion channel">ligand-gated ion channels</a>, specifically the nACh receptors and the glycine receptor, are <a href="/wiki/Dose-dependent" class="mw-redirect" title="Dose-dependent">dose-dependent</a>, with potentiation <i>or</i> inhibition occurring dependent on ethanol concentration. This is because ethanol's effects on these channels are a summation of positive and negative allosteric modulatory actions.<sup id="cite_ref-pmid19342616_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid19342616-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pharmacokinetics">Pharmacokinetics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Pharmacokinetics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The removal of ethanol from the human body, through oxidation by <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_dehydrogenase" title="Alcohol dehydrogenase">alcohol dehydrogenase</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Liver" title="Liver">liver</a>, is limited. Hence, the removal of a large concentration of alcohol from <a href="/wiki/Blood" title="Blood">blood</a> may follow <a href="/wiki/Zero_order_kinetics" class="mw-redirect" title="Zero order kinetics">zero-order kinetics</a>. This means that alcohol leaves the body at a constant rate, rather than having an elimination <a href="/wiki/Biological_half-life" title="Biological half-life">half-life</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-pmid5457514_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid5457514-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>The rate-limiting steps for one substance may be in common with other substances. As a result, the blood alcohol concentration can be used to modify the rate of metabolism of <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ethylene_glycol" title="Ethylene glycol">ethylene glycol</a>. Methanol itself is not highly toxic, but its metabolites <a href="/wiki/Formaldehyde" title="Formaldehyde">formaldehyde</a> and <a href="/wiki/Formic_acid" title="Formic acid">formic acid</a> are; therefore, to reduce the rate of production and concentration of these harmful metabolites, ethanol can be ingested.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ethylene_glycol" title="Ethylene glycol">Ethylene glycol</a> poisoning can be treated in the same way.</p>
<p>Pure ethanol will irritate the skin and eyes.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> Nausea, <a href="/wiki/Vomiting" title="Vomiting">vomiting</a> and intoxication are symptoms of ingestion. Long-term use by ingestion can result in serious liver damage.<sup id="cite_ref-msdset_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-msdset-84">[84]</a></sup> Atmospheric concentrations above one in a thousand are above the European Union <a href="/wiki/Occupational_exposure_limit" title="Occupational exposure limit">Occupational exposure limits</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-msdset_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-msdset-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Metabolism">Metabolism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Metabolism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_metabolism" title="Ethanol metabolism">Ethanol metabolism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_dehydrogenase" title="Alcohol dehydrogenase">Alcohol dehydrogenase</a></div>
<p>Ethanol within the human body is converted into <a href="/wiki/Acetaldehyde" title="Acetaldehyde">acetaldehyde</a> by <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_dehydrogenase" title="Alcohol dehydrogenase">alcohol dehydrogenase</a> and then into the <a href="/wiki/Acetyl" class="mw-redirect" title="Acetyl">acetyl</a> in <a href="/wiki/Acetyl_CoA" class="mw-redirect" title="Acetyl CoA">acetyl CoA</a> by <a href="/wiki/Acetaldehyde_dehydrogenase" title="Acetaldehyde dehydrogenase">acetaldehyde dehydrogenase</a>. <a href="/wiki/Acetyl_CoA" class="mw-redirect" title="Acetyl CoA">Acetyl CoA</a> is the final product of both carbohydrate and fat metabolism, where the acetyl can be further used to produce energy or for biosynthesis. As such, ethanol can be compared to an energy-bearing macronutrient, yielding approximately 7 kcal per gram consumed.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> However, the product of the first step of this breakdown, acetaldehyde,<sup id="cite_ref-boggan1_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boggan1-86">[86]</a></sup> is more toxic than ethanol. Acetaldehyde is linked to most of the clinical effects of alcohol. It has been shown to increase the risk of developing cirrhosis of the liver<sup id="cite_ref-boggan2_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-boggan2-87">[87]</a></sup> and multiple forms of cancer.</p>
<p>During the metabolism of alcohol via the respective dehydrogenases, NAD (<a href="/wiki/Nicotinamide_adenine_dinucleotide" title="Nicotinamide adenine dinucleotide">Nicotinamide adenine dinucleotide</a>) is converted into reduced NAD. Normally, NAD is used to metabolise fats in the liver, and as such alcohol competes with these fats for the use of NAD. Prolonged exposure to alcohol means that fats accumulate in the liver, leading to the term 'fatty liver'. Continued consumption (such as in <a href="/wiki/Alcoholism" title="Alcoholism">alcoholism</a>) then leads to cell death in the hepatocytes as the fat stores reduce the function of the cell to the point of death. These cells are then replaced with scar tissue, leading to the condition called <a href="/wiki/Cirrhosis" title="Cirrhosis">cirrhosis</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Alcohol_and_digestion">Alcohol and digestion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Alcohol and digestion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>A part of ethyl alcohol is hydrophobic. This hydrophobic or lipophilic end can diffuse across cells that line the stomach wall. In fact, alcohol is one of the rare substances that can be absorbed in the stomach. Most food substances are absorbed in the small intestine. However, even though alcohol can be absorbed in the stomach, it is mostly absorbed in the small intestine because the small intestine has a large surface area that promotes absorption. Once alcohol is absorbed in the small intestine, it delays the release of stomach contents from emptying into the small intestine. Thus, alcohol can delay the rate of absorption of nutrients.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> After absorption, alcohol reaches the liver where it is metabolized.</p>
<h5><span class="mw-headline" id="Breathalyzers">Breathalyzers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Breathalyzers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h5>
<p>Alcohol that is not processed by the liver goes to the heart. The liver can process only a certain amount of alcohol per unit time. Thus, when a person drinks too much alcohol, more alcohol can reach the heart. In the heart, alcohol reduces the force of heart contractions. Consequently, the heart will pump less blood, lowering overall body blood pressure.<sup id="cite_ref-body_alcohol_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-body_alcohol-53">[53]</a></sup> Also, blood that reaches the heart goes to the lungs to replenish blood's oxygen concentration. It is at this stage that a person can breathe out traces of alcohol.<sup id="cite_ref-body_alcohol_53-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-body_alcohol-53">[53]</a></sup> This is the underlying principle of the alcohol breath testing (or breathalyzers) to determine if a driver has been drinking and driving.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>From the lungs, blood returns to the heart and will be distributed throughout the body. Interestingly, alcohol increases levels of high-density lipoproteins(HDLs), which carry cholesterol.<sup id="cite_ref-body_alcohol_53-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-body_alcohol-53">[53]</a></sup> Alcohol is known to make blood less likely to clot, reducing risk of heart attack and stroke. This could be the reason that alcohol seems to produce health benefits when consumed in moderate amounts.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> Also, alcohol dilates blood vessels. Consequently, a person will feel warmer, and his/her skin flush and appear pink.<sup id="cite_ref-body_alcohol_53-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-body_alcohol-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Magnitude_of_effects">Magnitude of effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Magnitude of effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Some individuals have less effective forms of one or both of the metabolizing enzymes, and can experience more severe symptoms from ethanol consumption than others. However, those having acquired <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_tolerance" title="Alcohol tolerance">alcohol tolerance</a> have a greater quantity of these enzymes, and metabolize ethanol more rapidly.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemistry">Chemistry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Chemistry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_(data_page)" title="Ethanol (data page)">Ethanol (data page)</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_formula">Chemical formula</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Chemical formula">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Ethanol is a 2-carbon alcohol. Its <a href="/wiki/Chemical_formula" title="Chemical formula">molecular formula</a> is CH<sub>3</sub>CH<sub>2</sub>OH. An alternative notation is CH<sub>3</sub>–CH<sub>2</sub>–OH, which indicates that the carbon of a <a href="/wiki/Methyl_group" title="Methyl group">methyl group</a> (CH<sub>3</sub>–) is attached to the carbon of a <a href="/wiki/Methylene_group" title="Methylene group">methylene group</a> (–CH<sub>2</sub>–), which is attached to the oxygen of a <a href="/wiki/Hydroxyl" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydroxyl">hydroxyl group (–OH)</a>. It is a constitutional <a href="/wiki/Isomer" title="Isomer">isomer</a> of <a href="/wiki/Dimethyl_ether" title="Dimethyl ether">dimethyl ether</a>. Ethanol is sometimes abbreviated as <b>EtOH</b>, using the common organic chemistry notation of representing the ethyl group (C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>5</sub>-) with <b>Et</b>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_properties">Physical properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Physical properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Spiritusflamme_mit_spektrum.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Spiritusflamme_mit_spektrum.png/170px-Spiritusflamme_mit_spektrum.png" width="170" height="257" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5d/Spiritusflamme_mit_spektrum.png 1.5x" data-file-width="214" data-file-height="324" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Spiritusflamme_mit_spektrum.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Ethanol burning with its spectrum depicted</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Ethanol is a volatile, colorless liquid that has a slight odor. It burns with a smokeless blue flame that is not always visible in normal light.</p>
<p>The physical properties of ethanol stem primarily from the presence of its <a href="/wiki/Hydroxyl" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydroxyl">hydroxyl</a> group and the shortness of its carbon chain. Ethanol's hydroxyl group is able to participate in hydrogen bonding, rendering it more viscous and less volatile than less polar organic compounds of similar molecular weight, such as <a href="/wiki/Propane" title="Propane">propane</a>.</p>
<p>Ethanol is slightly more refractive than water, having a <a href="/wiki/Refractive_index" title="Refractive index">refractive index</a> of 1.36242 (at λ=589.3&#160;nm and 18.35&#160;°C or 65.03&#160;°F).<sup id="cite_ref-crc_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-crc-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Triple_point" title="Triple point">triple point</a> for ethanol is <span class="nowrap">150 <a href="/wiki/Kelvin" title="Kelvin">K</a></span> at a pressure of <span class="nowrap">4.3 × 10<sup>−4</sup> <a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">Pa</a></span>.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Solvent_properties">Solvent properties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Solvent properties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Ethanol is a versatile solvent, <a href="/wiki/Miscible" class="mw-redirect" title="Miscible">miscible</a> with water and with many organic solvents, including <a href="/wiki/Acetic_acid" title="Acetic acid">acetic acid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acetone" title="Acetone">acetone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Benzene" title="Benzene">benzene</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_tetrachloride" title="Carbon tetrachloride">carbon tetrachloride</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chloroform" title="Chloroform">chloroform</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diethyl_ether" title="Diethyl ether">diethyl ether</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethylene_glycol" title="Ethylene glycol">ethylene glycol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Glycerol" title="Glycerol">glycerol</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nitromethane" title="Nitromethane">nitromethane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pyridine" title="Pyridine">pyridine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Toluene" title="Toluene">toluene</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-crc_92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-crc-92">[92]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-merck_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merck-94">[94]</a></sup> It is also miscible with light aliphatic hydrocarbons, such as <a href="/wiki/Pentane" title="Pentane">pentane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hexane" title="Hexane">hexane</a>, and with aliphatic chlorides such as <a href="/wiki/1,1,1-Trichloroethane" title="1,1,1-Trichloroethane">trichloroethane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tetrachloroethylene" title="Tetrachloroethylene">tetrachloroethylene</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-merck_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merck-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<p>Ethanol's miscibility with water contrasts with the immiscibility of longer-chain alcohols (five or more carbon atoms), whose water miscibility decreases sharply as the number of carbons increases.<sup id="cite_ref-m_and_b_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-m_and_b-95">[95]</a></sup> The miscibility of ethanol with <a href="/wiki/Alkane" title="Alkane">alkanes</a> is limited to alkanes up to <a href="/wiki/Undecane" title="Undecane">undecane</a>: mixtures with <a href="/wiki/Dodecane" title="Dodecane">dodecane</a> and higher alkanes show a <a href="/wiki/Miscibility_gap" title="Miscibility gap">miscibility gap</a> below a certain temperature (about 13&#160;°C for dodecane<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>). The miscibility gap tends to get wider with higher alkanes and the temperature for complete miscibility increases.</p>
<p>Ethanol-water mixtures have less volume than the sum of their individual components at the given fractions. Mixing equal volumes of ethanol and water results in only 1.92 volumes of mixture.<sup id="cite_ref-crc_92-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-crc-92">[92]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ChemTech_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ChemTech-97">[97]</a></sup> Mixing ethanol and water is <a href="/wiki/Exothermic" class="mw-redirect" title="Exothermic">exothermic</a>, with up to 777&#160;J/mol<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> being released at 298&#160;K.</p>
<p>Mixtures of ethanol and water form an <a href="/wiki/Azeotrope" title="Azeotrope">azeotrope</a> at about 89 mole-% ethanol and 11 mole-% water<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> or a mixture of 95.6 percent ethanol by mass (or about 97% <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_by_volume" title="Alcohol by volume">alcohol by volume</a>) at normal pressure, which boils at 351K (78&#160;°C). This azeotropic composition is strongly temperature- and pressure-dependent and vanishes at temperatures below 303&#160;K.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ethanol-xtal-1976-3D-balls.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Ethanol-xtal-1976-3D-balls.png/300px-Ethanol-xtal-1976-3D-balls.png" width="300" height="222" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Ethanol-xtal-1976-3D-balls.png/450px-Ethanol-xtal-1976-3D-balls.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Ethanol-xtal-1976-3D-balls.png/600px-Ethanol-xtal-1976-3D-balls.png 2x" data-file-width="1100" data-file-height="814" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ethanol-xtal-1976-3D-balls.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Hydrogen bonding in solid ethanol at −186&#160;°C</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Hydrogen bonding causes pure ethanol to be <a href="/wiki/Hygroscopic" class="mw-redirect" title="Hygroscopic">hygroscopic</a> to the extent that it readily absorbs water from the air. The polar nature of the hydroxyl group causes ethanol to dissolve many ionic compounds, notably <a href="/wiki/Sodium_hydroxide" title="Sodium hydroxide">sodium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Potassium_hydroxide" title="Potassium hydroxide">potassium hydroxides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Magnesium_chloride" title="Magnesium chloride">magnesium chloride</a>, <a href="/wiki/Calcium_chloride" title="Calcium chloride">calcium chloride</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ammonium_chloride" title="Ammonium chloride">ammonium chloride</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ammonium_bromide" title="Ammonium bromide">ammonium bromide</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sodium_bromide" title="Sodium bromide">sodium bromide</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-merck_94-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merck-94">[94]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sodium_chloride" title="Sodium chloride">Sodium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Potassium_chloride" title="Potassium chloride">potassium chlorides</a> are slightly soluble in ethanol.<sup id="cite_ref-merck_94-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merck-94">[94]</a></sup> Because the ethanol molecule also has a nonpolar end, it will also dissolve nonpolar substances, including most <a href="/wiki/Essential_oil" title="Essential oil">essential oils</a><sup id="cite_ref-merckoils_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-merckoils-101">[101]</a></sup> and numerous flavoring, coloring, and medicinal agents.</p>
<p>The addition of even a few percent of ethanol to water sharply reduces the <a href="/wiki/Surface_tension" title="Surface tension">surface tension</a> of water. This property partially explains the "<a href="/wiki/Tears_of_wine" title="Tears of wine">tears of wine</a>" phenomenon. When wine is swirled in a glass, ethanol evaporates quickly from the thin film of wine on the wall of the glass. As the wine's ethanol content decreases, its surface tension increases and the thin film "beads up" and runs down the glass in channels rather than as a smooth sheet.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flammability">Flammability</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ethanol&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Flammability">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>An ethanol-water solution that contains 40% alcohol by weight will catch fire if heated to about 26&#160;°C (79&#160;°F) and if an ignition source is applied to it. This is called its <a href="/wiki/Flash_point" title="Flash point">flash point</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-flash_point_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-flash_point-102">[102]</a></sup> The flash point of pure ethanol is 16.60&#160;°C (61.88&#160;°F), less than average room temperature.</p>
<table class="wikitable" style="white-space: nowrap; text-align: center;">
